title="Yemen">Yemen</a>: Article 2 of the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Yemen" title="Constitution of Yemen">Constitution of Yemen</a>: "Islam is the religion of the state, and Arabic is its official Language."<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Status_of_religion_in_Israel">Status of religion in Israel</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=State_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Status of religion in Israel">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Jewish_state" title="Jewish state">Jewish state</a></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a> is defined in several of its laws as a "<a href="/wiki/Jewish_and_democratic_state" title="Jewish and democratic state">Jewish and democratic state</a>" (<i>medina yehudit ve-demokratit</i>). However, the term "<a href="/wiki/Jew" class="mw-redirect" title="Jew">Jewish</a>" is a <a href="/wiki/Polysemy" title="Polysemy">polyseme</a> that can describe the Jewish people <a href="/wiki/Who_is_a_Jew%3F" title="Who is a Jew?">as either an ethnic or a religious group</a>. The debate about the meaning of the term "Jewish" and its legal and social applications is one of the most profound issues with which Israeli society deals. The problem of the status of religion in Israel, even though it is relevant to all religions, usually refers to the status of <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a> in Israeli society. Thus, even though from a constitutional point of view Judaism is not the state religion in Israel, its status nevertheless determines relations between religion and state and the extent to which religion influences the political center.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The State of Israel supports religious institutions, particularly <a href="/wiki/Orthodox_Judaism" title="Orthodox Judaism">Orthodox Jewish</a> ones, and recognizes the "religious communities" as carried over from those recognized under the British Mandate\xe2\x80\x94in turn derived from the pre-1917 Ottoman system of <i><a href="/wiki/Millet_(Ottoman_Empire)" title="Millet (Ottoman Empire)">millets</a></i>. These are: Jewish and Christian (Eastern Orthodox, Latin [Catholic], Gregorian-Armenian, Armenian-Catholic, Syrian [Catholic], Chaldean [Uniate], Greek Catholic Melkite, Maronite, and Syrian Orthodox). The fact that the Muslim population was not defined as a religious community does not affect the rights of the Muslim community to practice their faith. At the end of the period covered by the 2009 U.S. International Religious Freedom Report, several of these denominations were pending official government recognition; however, the Government has allowed adherents of not officially recognized groups freedom to practice. In 1961, legislation gave Muslim Shari\'a courts exclusive jurisdiction in matters of personal status. Three additional religious communities have subsequently been recognized by Israeli law: the <a href="/wiki/Druze" title="Druze">Druze</a> (prior under Islamic jurisdiction), the Evangelical Episcopal Church, and the <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%27%C3%AD_Faith" title="Bah\xc3\xa1&#39;\xc3\xad Faith">Bah\xc3\xa1\'\xc3\xad</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> These groups have their own religious courts as official state courts for personal status matters (see <a href="/wiki/Millet_(Ottoman_Empire)" title="Millet (Ottoman Empire)">millet system</a>).\n</p><p>The structure and goals of the <a href="/wiki/Chief_Rabbinate_of_Israel" title="Chief Rabbinate of Israel">Chief Rabbinate of Israel</a> are governed by Israeli law, but the law does not say explicitly that it is a state Rabbinate. However, outspoken Israeli secularists such as <a href="/wiki/Shulamit_Aloni" title="Shulamit Aloni">Shulamit Aloni</a> and <a href="/wiki/Uri_Avnery" title="Uri Avnery">Uri Avnery</a> have long maintained that it is that in practice. Non-recognition of other streams of Judaism such as <a href="/wiki/Reform_Judaism" title="Reform Judaism">Reform Judaism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Judaism" title="Conservative Judaism">Conservative Judaism</a> is the cause of some controversy; rabbis belonging to these currents are not recognized as such by state institutions and marriages performed by them are not recognized as valid. As pointed out by Avnery and Aloni, the essential problem is that Israel carries on the top-down Ottoman <i>millet</i> system, under which the government reserves the complete discretion of recognizing some religions groups and not recognizing others. As of  2015<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=State_religion&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Marriage_in_Israel" title="Marriage in Israel">marriage in Israel</a> provides no provision for <a href="/wiki/Civil_marriage" title="Civil marriage">civil marriage</a>, marriage between people of different religions, marriages by people who do not belong to one of nine recognised religious communities, or <a href="/wiki/Same-sex_marriage" title="Same-sex marriage">same-sex marriages</a>, although there is recognition of marriages performed abroad.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_religions">Political religions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=State_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Political religions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In some countries, there is a <a href="/wiki/List_of_political_ideologies" title="List of political ideologies">political ideology</a> sponsored by the government that may be called <a href="/wiki/Political_religion" class="mw-redirect" title="Political religion">political religion</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Flag_of_North_Korea.svg/23px-Flag_of_North_Korea.svg.png" width="23" height="12" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Flag_of_North_Korea.svg/35px-Flag_of_North_Korea.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Flag_of_North_Korea.svg/46px-Flag_of_North_Korea.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="800" />&#160;</span><a href="/wiki/North_Korea" title="North Korea">North Korea</a>: the North Korean government has promulgated <i><a href="/wiki/Juche" title="Juche">Juche</a></i> as a political alternative to traditional religion. The doctrine advocates a strong nationalist propaganda basis and is fundamentally opposed to <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>, the two largest religions on the Korean peninsula. <i>Juche</i> theoreticians have, however, incorporated religious ideas into the state ideology. According to government figures, <i>Juche</i> is the largest political religion in <a href="/wiki/North_Korea" title="North Korea">North Korea</a>. The public practice of all other religions is overseen and subject to heavy surveillance by the state.</li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Additional_notes">Additional notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=State_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Additional notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<ul><li><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Flag_of_Indonesia.svg/23px-Flag_of_Indonesia.svg.png" width="23" height="15" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Flag_of_Indonesia.svg/35px-Flag_of_Indonesia.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Flag_of_Indonesia.svg/45px-Flag_of_Indonesia.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="450" data-file-height="300" />&#160;</span><a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a> does not declare or designate a state religion. However, <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Indonesia" title="Government of Indonesia">the government</a> only recognizes six religions: <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestantism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Catholicism" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholicism">Catholicism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucianism</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Indonesia" title="Constitution of Indonesia">Constitution of Indonesia</a> guarantees freedom of religion and the practice of other religions and beliefs, including the <a href="/wiki/Animism" title="Animism">animistic</a> indigenous ones, is not prohibited by any laws. Indonesians practicing traditional polytheistic and animists as well as <a href="/wiki/Sikhs" class="mw-redirect" title="Sikhs">Sikhs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jains" class="mw-redirect" title="Jains">Jains</a> are often counted as "Hindu" for government purposes.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> <a href="/wiki/Atheism" title="Atheism">Atheism</a>, although not prosecuted, is discouraged by the state ideology of <i><a href="/wiki/Pancasila_(politics)" title="Pancasila (politics)">Pancasila</a></i>. In addition, the province of <a href="/wiki/Aceh" title="Aceh">Aceh</a> receives a special status and a higher degree of autonomy, in which it may enact laws (<i><a href="/wiki/Qanun_(law)" title="Qanun (law)">qanuns</a></i>) based on the <a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">Sharia</a> and enforce it, especially to its Muslim residents.</li>\n<li><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Flag_of_Singapore.svg/23px-Flag_of_Singapore.svg.png" width="23" height="15" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Flag_of_Singapore.svg/35px-Flag_of_Singapore.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Flag_of_Singapore.svg/45px-Flag_of_Singapore.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="4320" data-file-height="2880" />&#160;</span><a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a> is officially a secular country and does not have a state religion, and has been named in one study as the "most religiously diverse nation in the world", with no religious group forming a majority.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>  That said the government gives official recognition to ten different religions, namely <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>, <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taoism" title="Taoism">Taoism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sikhism" title="Sikhism">Sikhism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">Zoroastrianism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jainism</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Baha%27i_Faith" class="mw-redirect" title="Baha&#39;i Faith">Baha\'i Faith</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>  and Singapore\'s penal code explicitly prohibits "wounding religious feelings". The <a href="/wiki/Jehovah%27s_Witnesses" title="Jehovah&#39;s Witnesses">Jehovah\'s Witnesses</a> and <a href="/wiki/Unification_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Unification Church">Unification Church</a> are also banned in Singapore, as the government deems them to be a threat to national security.</li>\n<li><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Flag_of_Nepal.svg/16px-Flag_of_Nepal.svg.png" width="16" height="20" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Flag_of_Nepal.svg/25px-Flag_of_Nepal.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Flag_of_Nepal.svg/33px-Flag_of_Nepal.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="726" data-file-height="885" />&#160;&#160;&#160;</span><a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a> was formerly the world\'s only Hindu kingdom, but ceased to be so in 2006 when Nepal became a democratic country and the new constitution declared it a <a href="/wiki/Secular_state" title="Secular state">secular state</a> and guaranteed the <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_religion" title="Freedom of religion">freedom of religion</a>.</li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Former_state_religions">Former state religions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=State_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Former state religions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-modern_era">Pre-modern era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=State_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Pre-modern era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Egypt_and_Sumer">Egypt and Sumer</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=State_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Egypt and Sumer">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_religion" title="History of religion">History of religion</a></div>\n<p>The concept of state religions was known as long ago as the empires of Egypt and <a href="/wiki/Sumer" title="Sumer">Sumer</a>, when every city state or people had its own god or gods. Many of the early Sumerian rulers were priests of their patron city god. Some of the earliest semi-mythological kings may have passed into the pantheon, like <a href="/wiki/Dumuzid_the_Shepherd" class="mw-redirect" title="Dumuzid the Shepherd">Dumuzid</a>, and some later kings came to be viewed as divine soon after their reigns, like <a href="/wiki/Sargon_the_Great" class="mw-redirect" title="Sargon the Great">Sargon the Great</a> of <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_Empire" title="Akkadian Empire">Akkad</a>. One of the first rulers to be proclaimed a god during his actual reign was <a href="/wiki/Gudea" title="Gudea">Gudea</a> of <a href="/wiki/Lagash" title="Lagash">Lagash</a>, followed by some later kings of <a href="/wiki/Ur" title="Ur">Ur</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Shulgi" title="Shulgi">Shulgi</a>. Often, the state religion was integral to the power base of the reigning government, such as in Egypt, where Pharaohs were often thought of as embodiments of the god Horus.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sassanid_Empire">Sassanid Empire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=State_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Sassanid Empire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">Zoroastrianism</a> was the state religion of the <a href="/wiki/Sassanid_dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Sassanid dynasty">Sassanid dynasty</a> which lasted until 651, when Persia was conquered by the <a href="/wiki/Rashidun_Caliphate" title="Rashidun Caliphate">Rashidun Caliphate</a>. However, it persisted as the state religion of the independent state of <a href="/wiki/Hyrcania" title="Hyrcania">Hyrcania</a> until the 15th century.\n</p><p>The tiny kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Adiabene" title="Adiabene">Adiabene</a> in <a href="/wiki/Northern_Mesopotamia" class="mw-redirect" title="Northern Mesopotamia">northern Mesopotamia</a> converted to <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a> around 34&#160;CE.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Greek_city-states">Greek city-states</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=State_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Greek city-states">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Many of the Greek city-states also had a god or goddess associated with that city. This would not be its only god or goddess, but the one that received special honors. In ancient Greece, the city of Athens had <a href="/wiki/Athena" title="Athena">Athena</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sparta" title="Sparta">Sparta</a> had <a href="/wiki/Ares" title="Ares">Ares</a>, <a href="/wiki/Delphi" title="Delphi">Delphi</a> had <a href="/wiki/Apollo" title="Apollo">Apollo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Artemis" title="Artemis">Artemis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Olympia,_Greece" title="Olympia, Greece">Olympia</a> had <a href="/wiki/Zeus" title="Zeus">Zeus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Corinth" title="Corinth">Corinth</a> had <a href="/wiki/Poseidon" title="Poseidon">Poseidon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thebes,_Greece" title="Thebes, Greece">Thebes</a> had <a href="/wiki/Demeter" title="Demeter">Demeter</a>.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Roman_religion_and_Christianity">Roman religion and Christianity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=State_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Roman religion and Christianity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>In Rome, the office of <i><a href="/wiki/Pontifex_Maximus" class="mw-redirect" title="Pontifex Maximus">Pontifex Maximus</a></i> came to be reserved for the Emperor, who was often <a href="/wiki/Apotheosis" title="Apotheosis">declared a god</a> posthumously, or sometimes during his reign. Failure to worship the Emperor as a god was at times punishable by death, as the Roman government sought to link emperor worship with loyalty to the Empire. Many Christians and Jews were subject to persecution, torture and death in the Roman Empire, because it was against their beliefs to worship the Emperor.\n</p><p>In 311, Emperor <a href="/wiki/Galerius" title="Galerius">Galerius</a>, on his deathbed, declared a religious indulgence to Christians throughout the Roman Empire, focusing on the ending of anti-Christian persecution. <a href="/wiki/Constantine_I" class="mw-redirect" title="Constantine I">Constantine I</a> and <a href="/wiki/Licinius" title="Licinius">Licinius</a>, the two <i><a href="/wiki/Augustus_(title)" title="Augustus (title)">Augusti</a></i>, by the <a href="/wiki/Edict_of_Milan" title="Edict of Milan">Edict of Milan</a> of 313, enacted a law allowing religious freedom to everyone within the Roman Empire. Furthermore, the Edict of Milan cited that Christians may openly practice their religion unmolested and unrestricted, and provided that properties taken from Christians be returned to them unconditionally. Although the Edict of Milan allowed religious freedom throughout the Empire, it did not abolish nor disestablish the Roman state cult (Roman polytheistic paganism). The Edict of Milan was written in such a way as to implore the blessings of the deity.\n</p><p>Constantine called up the <a href="/wiki/First_Council_of_Nicaea" title="First Council of Nicaea">First Council of Nicaea</a> in 325, although he was not a baptised Christian until years later. Despite enjoying considerable popular support, Christianity was still not the official state religion in Rome, although it was in some neighboring states such as <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Armenia_(antiquity)" title="Kingdom of Armenia (antiquity)">Armenia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Iberia" title="Kingdom of Iberia">Iberia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Aksum" title="Kingdom of Aksum">Aksum</a>.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Religion_in_ancient_Rome" title="Religion in ancient Rome">Roman Religion</a> (<a href="/wiki/Neoplatonic" class="mw-redirect" title="Neoplatonic">Neoplatonic</a> <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_ancient_Greece" class="mw-redirect" title="Religion in ancient Greece">Hellenism</a>) was restored for a time by the Emperor <a href="/wiki/Julian_(emperor)" title="Julian (emperor)">Julian</a> from 361 to 363. Julian does not appear to have reinstated the persecutions of the earlier <a href="/wiki/Roman_emperors" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman emperors">Roman emperors</a>.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholic">Catholic</a> Christianity, as opposed to Arianism and other ideologies deemed <a href="/wiki/Heresy" title="Heresy">heretical</a>, was declared to be the <a href="/wiki/State_church_of_the_Roman_Empire" title="State church of the Roman Empire">state religion of the Roman Empire</a> on 27 February 380<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> by the decree <i><a href="/wiki/Edict_of_Thessalonica" title="Edict of Thessalonica">De fide catolica</a></i> of Emperor <a href="/wiki/Theodosius_I" title="Theodosius I">Theodosius I</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Han_dynasty_Confucianism">Han dynasty Confucianism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=State_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Han dynasty Confucianism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>In China, the <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a> (206 BCE \xe2\x80\x93 220 CE) advocated <a href="/wiki/Confucianism" title="Confucianism">Confucianism</a> as the <i>de facto</i> state religion, establishing tests based on Confucian texts as an entrance requirement into government service\xe2\x80\x94although, in fact, the "Confucianism" advocated by the Han emperors may be more properly termed a sort of Confucian <a href="/wiki/Legalism_(Chinese_philosophy)" title="Legalism (Chinese philosophy)">Legalism</a> or "State Confucianism". This sort of Confucianism continued to be regarded by the emperors, with a few notable exceptions, as a form of state religion from this time until the overthrow of the imperial system of government in 1911.  Note however, there is a debate over whether Confucianism (including Neo-confucianism) is a religion or purely a philosophical system.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Yuan_dynasty_Buddhism">Yuan dynasty Buddhism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=State_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Yuan dynasty Buddhism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>During the Mongol <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">Yuan dynasty</a> (1271\xe2\x80\x931368 CE), <a href="/wiki/Tibetan_Buddhism" title="Tibetan Buddhism">Tibetan Buddhism</a> was established as the <i>de facto</i> state religion by the Mongol ruler <a href="/wiki/Kublai_Khan" title="Kublai Khan">Kublai Khan</a>, the founder of the Yuan dynasty. The top-level department and government agency known as the <a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Buddhist_and_Tibetan_Affairs" title="Bureau of Buddhist and Tibetan Affairs">Bureau of Buddhist and Tibetan Affairs</a> (Xuanzheng Yuan) was set up in <a href="/wiki/Khanbaliq" title="Khanbaliq">Khanbaliq</a> (modern <a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">Beijing</a>) to supervise <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_monk" class="mw-redirect" title="Buddhist monk">Buddhist monks</a> throughout the empire. Since Kublai Khan only esteemed the <a href="/wiki/Sakya" title="Sakya">Sakya</a> sect of Tibetan Buddhism, other religions became less important. Before the end of the Yuan dynasty, 14 leader of the Sakya sect had held the post of <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Preceptor" title="Imperial Preceptor">Imperial Preceptor</a> (Dishi), thereby enjoy special power.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Golden_Horde_and_Ilkhanate">Golden Horde and Ilkhanate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=State_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Golden Horde and Ilkhanate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Shamanism" title="Shamanism">Shamanism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> were once the dominant religions among the ruling class of the Mongol khanates of <a href="/wiki/Golden_Horde" title="Golden Horde">Golden Horde</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ilkhanate" title="Ilkhanate">Ilkhanate</a>, the two western khanates of the <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">Mongol Empire</a>. In the early days, the rulers of both khanates increasingly adopted <a href="/wiki/Tibetan_Buddhism" title="Tibetan Buddhism">Tibetan Buddhism</a>, similar to the Yuan dynasty at that time. However, the Mongol rulers <a href="/wiki/Ghazan" title="Ghazan">Ghazan</a> of Ilkhanate and <a href="/wiki/%C3%96z_Beg_Khan" title="\xc3\x96z Beg Khan">Uzbeg</a> of Golden Horde converted to <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> in 1295 CE because of the Muslim Mongol emir <a href="/wiki/Nawr%C5%ABz_(Mongol_emir)" title="Nawr\xc5\xabz (Mongol emir)">Nawruz</a> and in 1313 CE because of <a href="/wiki/Sufi" class="mw-redirect" title="Sufi">Sufi</a> <a href="/wiki/Bukhara" title="Bukhara">Bukharan</a> <a href="/wiki/Sayyid" title="Sayyid">sayyid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sheikh" title="Sheikh">sheikh</a> <a href="/w/index.php?title=Ibn_Abdul_Hamid&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Ibn Abdul Hamid (page does not exist)">Ibn Abdul Hamid</a> respectively. Their official favoring of Islam as the state religion coincided with a marked attempt to bring the regime closer to the non-Mongol majority of the regions they ruled. In Ilkhanate, <a href="/wiki/Christian" title="Christian">Christian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jewish" class="mw-redirect" title="Jewish">Jewish</a> subjects lost their equal status with Muslims and again had to pay the poll tax; <a href="/wiki/Buddhists" class="mw-redirect" title="Buddhists">Buddhists</a> had the starker choice of conversion or expulsion.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> In Golden Horde, Buddhism and Shamanism among the Mongols were proscribed, and by 1315, Uzbeg had successfully Islamicized the Horde, killing Jochid princes and Buddhist <a href="/wiki/Lama" title="Lama">lamas</a> who opposed his religious policy and succession of the throne.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_era">Modern era</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=State_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Modern era">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-globalize" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Europe">Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=State_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<ul><li><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Flag_of_the_Netherlands.svg/23px-Flag_of_the_Netherlands.svg.png" width="23" height="15" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Flag_of_the_Netherlands.svg/35px-Flag_of_the_Netherlands.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Flag_of_the_Netherlands.svg/45px-Flag_of_the_Netherlands.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="600" />&#160;</span><a href="/wiki/Netherlands" title="Netherlands">Netherlands</a>: Article 133 of the 1814 <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_the_Netherlands" title="Constitution of the Netherlands">Constitution</a> stipulated the <a href="/wiki/King_of_the_Netherlands" class="mw-redirect" title="King of the Netherlands">Sovereign Prince</a> should be a member of the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Reformed_Church" title="Dutch Reformed Church">Reformed Church</a>; this provision was dropped in the 1815 Constitution.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> The 1815 Constitution also provided for a state salary and pension for the priesthood of established religions at the time (Protestantism, Catholicism and Judaism). This settlement, nicknamed <i>de zilveren koorde</i> (the silver cord), was abolished in 1983.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Flag_of_Norway.svg/21px-Flag_of_Norway.svg.png" width="21" height="15" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Flag_of_Norway.svg/32px-Flag_of_Norway.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Flag_of_Norway.svg/41px-Flag_of_Norway.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1100" data-file-height="800" />&#160;</span><a href="/wiki/Norway" title="Norway">Norway</a>: Paragraph 4 in the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Norway" title="Constitution of Norway">Constitution of Norway</a> states that the Sovereign <a href="/wiki/Monarch" title="Monarch">Monarch</a> must be confessing the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Norway" title="Church of Norway">Lutheran Evangelical Religion</a>. As of 2012 the Constitution of Norway no longer names Lutheranism as the official religion of the state,<sup id="cite_ref-snl.no_31-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-snl.no-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> but article 16 says that "The <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Norway" title="Church of Norway">Church of Norway</a> [...] will remain the Established Church of Norway and will as such be supported by the State."<sup id="cite_ref-:0_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> As of 1 January 2017 the Church of Norway is a legal entity independent of the state.<sup id="cite_ref-regjeringen.no_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-regjeringen.no-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-stortinget.no_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stortinget.no-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Asia">Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=State_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<ul><li><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Flag_of_Japan_%281870%E2%80%931999%29.svg/22px-Flag_of_Japan_%281870%E2%80%931999%29.svg.png" width="22" height="15" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Flag_of_Japan_%281870%E2%80%931999%29.svg/33px-Flag_of_Japan_%281870%E2%80%931999%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Flag_of_Japan_%281870%E2%80%931999%29.svg/43px-Flag_of_Japan_%281870%E2%80%931999%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="700" />&#160;</span><a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Japan" title="Empire of Japan">Empire of Japan</a>:<a href="/wiki/State_Shinto" title="State Shinto">State Shinto</a>, From 1890 to 1947.</li></ul>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Former_state_churches_in_British_North_America">Former state churches in British North America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=State_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Former state churches in British North America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Protestant_colonies">Protestant colonies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=State_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Protestant colonies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<ul><li>The colonies of <a href="/wiki/Plymouth_Colony" title="Plymouth Colony">Plymouth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Bay_Colony" title="Massachusetts Bay Colony">Massachusetts Bay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Connecticut_Colony" title="Connecticut Colony">Connecticut</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Haven_Colony" title="New Haven Colony">New Haven</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Province_of_New_Hampshire" title="Province of New Hampshire">New Hampshire</a> were founded by <a href="/wiki/Puritan" class="mw-redirect" title="Puritan">Puritan</a> Calvinist Protestants, and had <a href="/wiki/Congregational_church" title="Congregational church">Congregational</a> established churches.</li>\n<li>The colonies of <a href="/wiki/Province_of_New_York" title="Province of New York">New York</a>, <a href="/wiki/Colony_of_Virginia" title="Colony of Virginia">Virginia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Province_of_North_Carolina" class="mw-redirect" title="Province of North Carolina">North Carolina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Province_of_South_Carolina" title="Province of South Carolina">South Carolina</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Georgia" title="Province of Georgia">Georgia</a> maintained the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a> as the established church.</li>\n<li>The <a href="/wiki/Colony_of_Maryland" class="mw-redirect" title="Colony of Maryland">Colony of Maryland</a> was founded by a charter granted in 1632 to George Calvert, secretary of state to Charles I, and his son Cecil, both recent converts to Roman Catholicism. Under their leadership many English Catholic gentry families settled in Maryland. However, the colonial government was officially neutral in religious affairs, granting toleration to all Christian groups and enjoining them to avoid actions which antagonized the others. On several occasions, low-church dissenters led insurrections which temporarily overthrew the Calvert rule. In 1689, when William and Mary <a href="/wiki/Glorious_Revolution" title="Glorious Revolution">came to the English throne</a>, they acceded to demands to revoke the original royal charter. In 1701, the Church of England was proclaimed, and in the course of the 18th century Maryland Catholics were first barred from public office, then disenfranchised, although not all of the laws passed against them (notably laws restricting property rights and imposing penalties for sending children to be educated in foreign Catholic institutions) were enforced, and some Catholics even continued to hold public office.</li></ul>\n<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Catholic_colonies">Catholic colonies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=State_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Catholic colonies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<ul><li>When <a href="/wiki/New_France" title="New France">New France</a> was transferred to Great Britain in 1763, the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic Church">Roman Catholic Church</a> remained under toleration, but <a href="/wiki/Huguenot" class="mw-redirect" title="Huguenot">Huguenots</a> were allowed entrance where they had formerly been banned from settlement by Parisian authorities.</li>\n<li>When <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Florida" title="Spanish Florida">Spanish Florida</a> was ceded to Great Britain in 1763, the British divided Florida into two colonies, East and West Florida, which both continued a policy of toleration for the Catholic residents.</li></ul>\n<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Colonies_with_no_established_church">Colonies with no established church</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=State_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Colonies with no established church">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>\n<ul><li>The <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Pennsylvania" title="Province of Pennsylvania">Province of Pennsylvania</a> was founded by <a href="/wiki/Religious_Society_of_Friends" class="mw-redirect" title="Religious Society of Friends">Quakers</a>, but the colony never had an established church.</li>\n<li>The <a href="/wiki/Province_of_New_Jersey" title="Province of New Jersey">Province of New Jersey</a>, without official religion, had a significant <a href="/wiki/Quaker" class="mw-redirect" title="Quaker">Quaker</a> lobby, but Calvinists of all types also had a presence.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Delaware_Colony" title="Delaware Colony">Delaware Colony</a> had no established church, but was contested between Catholics and Quakers.</li>\n<li>The <a href="/wiki/Colony_of_Rhode_Island_and_Providence_Plantations" title="Colony of Rhode Island and Providence Plantations">Colony of Rhode Island and Providence Plantations</a>, founded by religious dissenters forced to flee the Massachusetts Bay colony, is widely regarded as the first polity to grant religious freedom to all its citizens, although Catholics were barred intermittently. Baptists, Seekers/Quakers and Jews made this colony their home.  The <a href="/wiki/Rhode_Island_Royal_Charter" title="Rhode Island Royal Charter">King Charles Charter</a> of 1663 guaranteed "full liberty in religious concernments".</li></ul>\n<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Tabular_summary">Tabular summary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;note 8&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/West_Indies" title="West Indies">West Indies</a></td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a></td>\n<td>1868 (Barbados, not until 1969)\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: close; -webkit-column-width: close; column-width: close; list-style-type: decimal;">\n<ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-95"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-95">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In several colonies, the establishment ceased to exist in practice at the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">Revolution</a>, about 1776;<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> this is the date of permanent legal abolition.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-97"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-97">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In 1789 the Georgia Constitution was amended as follows:\n"Article IV. Section 10. No person within this state shall, upon any pretense, be deprived of the inestimable privilege of worshipping God in any manner agreeable to his own conscience, nor be compelled to attend any place of worship contrary to his own faith and judgment; nor shall he ever be obliged to pay tithes, taxes, or any other rate, for the building or repairing any place of worship, or for the maintenance of any minister or ministry, contrary to what he believes to be right, or hath voluntarily engaged. To do. No one religious society shall ever be established in this state, in preference to another; nor shall any person be denied the enjoyment of any civil right merely on account of his religious principles."</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-98"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-98">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">From 1780 to 1824, Massachusetts residents were all required to attend a <a href="/wiki/Parish_church" title="Parish church">parish church</a>, the denomination of which was chosen by majority vote of town residents, but in effect this de facto established Congregationalism as the state religion. For details see <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Massachusetts#Declaration_of_Rights" title="Constitution of Massachusetts">Constitution of Massachusetts</a>.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-99"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-99">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Until 1877 the New Hampshire Constitution required members of the State legislature to be of the Protestant religion. Until 1968 the Constitution allowed for state funding of Protestant classrooms but not Catholic classrooms.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-100"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-100">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The North Carolina Constitution of 1776 disestablished the Anglican church, but until 1835 the NC Constitution allowed only Protestants to hold public office. From 1835\xe2\x80\x931876 it allowed only Christians (including Catholics) to hold public office. Article VI, Section 8 of the current NC Constitution forbids "any person who shall deny the being of Almighty God" from holding public office. Such clauses were held by the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">United States Supreme Court</a> to be unenforceable in the 1961 case of <i><a href="/wiki/Torcaso_v._Watkins" title="Torcaso v. Watkins">Torcaso v. Watkins</a></i>, when the court ruled unanimously that the <a href="/wiki/First_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="First Amendment to the United States Constitution">First</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fourteenth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Fourteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution">Fourteenth</a> Amendment protections prohibiting federal religious tests also applied to the states under the doctrine of <a href="/wiki/Incorporation_of_the_Bill_of_Rights" title="Incorporation of the Bill of Rights">incorporation</a>.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-WEFL-101"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-WEFL_101-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-WEFL_101-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">\nReligious tolerance for Catholics with an established Church of England was policy in the former Spanish Colonies of East and West Florida while under British rule.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-EWFL-102"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-EWFL_102-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-EWFL_102-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">In <a href="/wiki/Peace_of_Paris_(1783)#Treaty_with_Spain" title="Peace of Paris (1783)">1783 Peace of Paris</a>, which ended the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">American Revolutionary War</a>, the British ceded both East and West Florida back to Spain (see <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Florida" title="Spanish Florida">Spanish Florida</a>).</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-103"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-103">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Tithes for the support of the Anglican Church in Virginia were suspended in 1776, and never restored. 1786 is the date of the <a href="/wiki/Virginia_Statute_of_Religious_Freedom" class="mw-redirect" title="Virginia Statute of Religious Freedom">Virginia Statute of Religious Freedom</a>, which prohibited any coercion to support any religious body.</span>\n</li>\n</ol></div>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-British_colonies">Non-British colonies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=State_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Non-British colonies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>These areas were disestablished and dissolved, yet their presences were tolerated by the English and later British colonial governments, as <a href="/wiki/Foreign_Protestants" title="Foreign Protestants">Foreign Protestants</a>, whose communities were expected to observe their own ways without causing controversy or conflict for the prevalent colonists. After the Revolution, their ethno-religious backgrounds were chiefly sought as the most compatible non-British Isles immigrants.\n</p>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/New_Netherland" title="New Netherland">New Netherland</a> was founded by <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Reformed" class="mw-redirect" title="Dutch Reformed">Dutch Reformed</a> Calvinists.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/New_Sweden" title="New Sweden">New Sweden</a> was founded by <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Sweden" title="Church of Sweden">Church of Sweden</a> Lutherans.</li></ul>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="State_of_Deseret">State of Deseret</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=State_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: State of Deseret">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/State_of_Deseret" title="State of Deseret">State of Deseret</a> was a provisional <a href="/wiki/U.S._state" title="U.S. state">state</a> of the United States, proposed in 1849, by <a href="/wiki/Mormon" class="mw-redirect" title="Mormon">Mormon</a> settlers in <a href="/wiki/Salt_Lake_City" title="Salt Lake City">Salt Lake City</a>. The provisional state existed for slightly over two years, but attempts to gain recognition by the United States government foundered for various reasons. The <a href="/wiki/Utah_Territory" title="Utah Territory">Utah Territory</a> which was then founded was under Mormon control, and repeated attempts to gain statehood met resistance, in part due to concerns over the principle of separation of church and state conflicting with the practice of members of <a href="/wiki/The_Church_of_Jesus_Christ_of_Latter-day_Saints" title="The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints">The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints</a> of placing their highest value on "following counsel" in virtually all matters relating to their church-centered lives. The state of <a href="/wiki/Utah" title="Utah">Utah</a> was eventually admitted to the union on 4 January 1896, after the various issues had been resolved.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Established_churches_and_former_state_churches">Established churches and former state churches</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=State_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Established churches and former title="Wales">Wales</a><sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;note 8&#93;</a></sup></td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a></td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Anglican" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglican">Anglican</a></td>\n<td>1920\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_W%C3%BCrttemberg" title="Kingdom of W\xc3\xbcrttemberg">W\xc3\xbcrttemberg</a></td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Evangelical_State_Church_in_W%C3%BCrttemberg" class="mw-redirect" title="Evangelical State Church in W\xc3\xbcrttemberg">Evangelical State Church in W\xc3\xbcrttemberg</a></td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Lutheran" class="mw-redirect" title="Lutheran">Lutheran</a></td>\n<td>1918\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=State_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Blasphemy_law" title="Blasphemy law">Blasphemy law</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Church_tax" title="Church tax">Church tax</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Civil_religion" title="Civil religion">Civil religion</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Confessional_state" title="Confessional state">Confessional state</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Divine_rule" class="mw-redirect" title="Divine rule">Divine rule</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Elite_religion" title="Elite religion">Elite religion</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Major_religious_groups" title="Major religious groups">Major religious groups</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Religious_toleration" class="mw-redirect" title="Religious toleration">Religious toleration</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Secular_religion" title="Secular religion">Secular religion</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Separation_of_church_and_state" title="Separation of church and state">Separation of church and state</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/State_Shinto" title="State Shinto">State Shinto</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Status_of_religious_freedom_by_country" class="mw-redirect" title="Status of religious freedom by country">Status of religious freedom by country</a></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=State_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">\n<ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-105"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-105">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.v-brazil.com/government/laws/titleIII.html">Brazilian Laws \xe2\x80\x93 the Federal Constitution \xe2\x80\x93 The Organization of State</a>. V-brazil.com. Retrieved 5 May 2012. Brazil had <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholicism" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholicism">Roman Catholicism</a> as the state religion from the country\'s <a href="/wiki/Independence_of_Brazil" title="Independence of Brazil">independence</a>, in 1822, until the fall of the <a href="/wiki/Brazilian_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Brazilian Empire">Brazilian Empire</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Rep%C3%BAblica_Velha" class="mw-redirect" title="Rep\xc3\xbablica Velha">new Republican government</a> passed, in 1890, Decree 119-A <cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.planalto.gov.br/ccivil_03/decreto/1851-1899/d119-a.htm">"Decreto 119-A"</a>. <q>Prohibits federal and state authorities to intervene on religion, granting freedom of religion.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Decreto+119-A&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.planalto.gov.br%2Fccivil_03%2Fdecreto%2F1851-1899%2Fd119-a.htm&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AState+religion" class="Z3988"></span><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r861714446">.mw-parser-output cite.citation{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output q{quotes:"\\"""\\"""\'""\'"}.mw-parser-output code.cs1-code{color:inherit;background:inherit;border:inherit;padding:inherit}.mw-parser-output .cs1-lock-free a{background:url("//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Lock-green.svg/9px-Lock-green.svg.png")no-repeat;background-position:right .1em center}.mw-parser-output .cs1-lock-limited a,.mw-parser-output .cs1-lock-registration a{background:url("//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Lock-gray-alt-2.svg/9px-Lock-gray-alt-2.svg.png")no-repeat;background-position:right .1em center}.mw-parser-output .cs1-lock-subscription a{background:url("//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Lock-red-alt-2.svg/9px-Lock-red-alt-2.svg.png")no-repeat;background-position:right .1em center}.mw-parser-output .cs1-subscription,.mw-parser-output .cs1-registration{color:#555}.mw-parser-output .cs1-subscription span,.mw-parser-output .cs1-registration span{border-bottom:1px dotted;cursor:help}.mw-parser-output .cs1-hidden-error{display:none;font-size:100%}.mw-parser-output .cs1-visible-error{font-size:100%}.mw-parser-output .cs1-subscription,.mw-parser-output .cs1-registration,.mw-parser-output .cs1-format{font-size:95%}.mw-parser-output .cs1-kern-left,.mw-parser-output .cs1-kern-wl-left{padding-left:0.2em}.mw-parser-output .cs1-kern-right,.mw-parser-output .cs1-kern-wl-right{padding-right:0.2em}</style> (still in force), instituting the separation of church and state for the first time in Brazilian law. <a href="/wiki/Positivist" class="mw-redirect" title="Positivist">Positivist</a> thinker <a href="/w/index.php?title=Dem%C3%A9trio_Nunes_Ribeiro&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Dem\xc3\xa9trio Nunes Ribeiro (page does not exist)">Dem\xc3\xa9trio Nunes Ribeiro</a> urged the new government to adopt this stance. The 1891 Constitution, the first under the Republican system of government, abolished privileges for any specific religion, reaffirming the separation of church and state. This has been the case ever since the 1988 <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Brazil" title="Constitution of Brazil">Constitution of Brazil</a>, currently in force, does so in its Nineteenth Article. The Preamble to the Constitution does refer to "God\'s protection" over the document\'s <a href="/wiki/Promulgation" title="Promulgation">promulgation</a>, but this is not legally taken as endorsement of belief in any deity.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-107"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-107">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In France the <a href="/wiki/Concordat_of_1801" title="Concordat of 1801">Concordat of 1801</a> made the Roman Catholic, <a href="/wiki/Calvinist" class="mw-redirect" title="Calvinist">Calvinist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lutheran" class="mw-redirect" title="Lutheran">Lutheran</a> churches state-sponsored religions, as well as <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a>.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-110"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-110">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In Hungary the constitutional laws of 1848 declared five established churches on equal status: the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic">Roman Catholic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Calvinist" class="mw-redirect" title="Calvinist">Calvinist</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lutheran" class="mw-redirect" title="Lutheran">Lutheran</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern Orthodox">Eastern Orthodox</a> and <a href="/wiki/Unitarianism" title="Unitarianism">Unitarian</a> Church. In 1868 the law was ratified again after the <a href="/wiki/Ausgleich" class="mw-redirect" title="Ausgleich">Ausgleich</a>. In 1895 <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a> was also recognized as the sixth established church. In 1948 every distinction between the different denominations were abolished.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-115"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-115">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Ireland" title="Kingdom of Ireland">Kingdom of Ireland</a> the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Ireland" title="Church of Ireland">Church of Ireland</a> was established <a href="/wiki/Reformation_in_Ireland" title="Reformation in Ireland">in the Reformation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-LivingstoneSparks2013_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LivingstoneSparks2013-111">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Union_1800" class="mw-redirect" title="Act of Union 1800">Act of Union 1800</a> created the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_of_Great_Britain_and_Ireland" title="United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland">United Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland</a> with the <a href="/wiki/United_Church_of_England_and_Ireland" class="mw-redirect" title="United Church of England and Ireland">United Church of England and Ireland</a> established outside Scotland. The <a href="/wiki/Irish_Church_Act_1869" title="Irish Church Act 1869">Irish Church Act 1869</a> demerged and disestablished the Church of Ireland,<sup id="cite_ref-LivingstoneSparks2013_111-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LivingstoneSparks2013-111">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> and the island was <a href="/wiki/Partition_of_Ireland" title="Partition of Ireland">partitioned in 1922</a>. The Republic of Ireland\'s <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Ireland" title="Constitution of Ireland">1937 constitution</a> prohibits an established religion.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> Originally, it recognized the "special position" of the Roman Catholic Church "as the guardian of the Faith professed by <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholicism_in_Ireland" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholicism in Ireland">the great majority of the citizens</a>", and recognized "the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Ireland" title="Church of Ireland">Church of Ireland</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian_Church_in_Ireland" title="Presbyterian Church in Ireland">Presbyterian Church in Ireland</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Methodist_Church_in_Ireland" title="Methodist Church in Ireland">Methodist Church in Ireland</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Religious_Society_of_Friends" class="mw-redirect" title="Religious Society of Friends">Religious Society of Friends</a> in Ireland, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Jews_in_Ireland" class="mw-redirect" title="Jews in Ireland">Jewish Congregations</a> and the other religious denominations existing in Ireland at the date of the coming into operation of this Constitution".<sup id="cite_ref-KeoghMcCarthy2007_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KeoghMcCarthy2007-113">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> These provisions were <a href="/wiki/Fifth_Amendment_of_the_Constitution_of_Ireland" title="Fifth Amendment of the Constitution of Ireland">deleted in 1973</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-120"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-120">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Philippines was among several possessions ceded by <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> to the United States in 1898; religious freedom was subsequently guaranteed in the archipelago. This was codified in the <a href="/wiki/Philippine_Organic_Act_(1902)" title="Philippine Organic Act (1902)">Philippine Organic Act (1902)</a>, section 5:  "... That no law shall be made respecting an establishment of religion or prohibiting the free exercise thereof, and that the free exercise and enjoyment of religious profession and worship, without discrimination or preference, shall forever be allowed." A similarly-worded provision still exists in the <a href="/wiki/1987_Constitution" class="mw-redirect" title="1987 Constitution">present Constitution</a>. Catholicism remains the predominant religion, wielding considerable political and cultural influence.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-121"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-121">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Article 25 of <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.sejm.gov.pl/prawo/konst/angielski/kon1.htm">the constitution</a> states: "1. Churches and other religious organizations shall have equal rights. 2. Public authorities in the Republic of Poland shall be impartial in matters of personal conviction". Article 114 of the Polish <a href="/wiki/March_Constitution_of_Poland" class="mw-redirect" title="March Constitution of Poland">March Constitution of 1921</a> declared the Roman Catholic Church to hold "the principal position among religious denominations equal before the law" (in reference to the idea of <i><a href="/wiki/Primus_inter_pares" title="Primus inter pares">first among equals</a></i>). The article was continued in force by article 81 of the <a href="/wiki/April_Constitution_of_Poland" title="April Constitution of Poland">April Constitution of 1935</a>. The Soviet-backed <a href="/wiki/PKWN_Manifesto" title="PKWN Manifesto">PKWN Manifesto</a> of 1944 reintroduced the March Constitution, which remained in force until it was replaced by the <a href="/wiki/Small_Constitution_of_1947" title="Small Constitution of 1947">Small Constitution of 1947</a>.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-127"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-127">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The <a href="/wiki/First_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="First Amendment to the United States Constitution">First Amendment</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Constitution of the United States">U.S. Constitution</a> explicitly forbids the <a href="/wiki/Federal_government_of_the_United_States" title="Federal government of the United States">federal government</a> from enacting any law respecting a religious establishment, and thus forbids either designating an official church for the United States, or interfering with <a href="/wiki/U.S._state" title="U.S. state">State</a> and local official churches\xe2\x80\x94which were common when the First Amendment was enacted. It did not prevent <a href="/wiki/State_government" title="State government">state governments</a> from establishing official churches. <a href="/wiki/Connecticut" title="Connecticut">Connecticut</a> continued to do so until it replaced its colonial <a href="/wiki/Fundamental_Orders_of_Connecticut" title="Fundamental Orders of Connecticut">Charter</a> with the <a href="/wiki/History_of_Connecticut#The_1818_Constitution_(1818\xe2\x80\x931890)" title="History of Connecticut">Connecticut Constitution of 1818</a>; Massachusetts retained an establishment of religion in general until 1833.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> As of  2010<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=State_religion&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, Article III of the Massachusetts constitution still provided, "... the legislature shall, from time to time, authorize and require, the several towns, parishes, precincts, and other bodies politic, or religious societies, to make suitable provision, at their own expense, for the institution of the public worship of God, and for the support and maintenance of public Protestant teachers of piety, religion and morality, in all cases where such provision shall not be made voluntarily."<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>\n\nThe <a href="/wiki/Fourteenth_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Fourteenth Amendment to the United States Constitution">Fourteenth Amendment</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Constitution of the United States">U.S. Constitution</a>, ratified in 1868, makes no mention of religious establishment, but forbids the states to "abridge the privileges or immunities" of U.S. citizens, or to "deprive any person of life, liberty, or property, without due process of law". In the 1947 case of <a href="/wiki/Everson_v._Board_of_Education" title="Everson v. Board of Education">Everson v. Board of Education</a>, the United States Supreme Court held that this later provision <a href="/wiki/Incorporation_(Bill_of_Rights)" class="mw-redirect" title="Incorporation (Bill of Rights)">incorporates</a> the First Amendment\'s Establishment Clause as applying to the States, and thereby prohibits state and local religious establishments. The exact boundaries of this prohibition are still disputed, and are a frequent source of cases before the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">U.S. Supreme Court</a>\xe2\x80\x94especially as the Court must now balance, on a state level, the First Amendment prohibitions on government establishment of official religions with the First Amendment prohibitions on government interference with the free exercise of religion. See <a href="/wiki/School_prayer" title="School prayer">school prayer</a> for such a controversy in contemporary American politics.\n\nAll current State constitutions do mention a Creator, but include guarantees of religious liberty parallel to the <a href="/wiki/First_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="First Amendment to the United States Constitution">First Amendment</a>. The constitutions of eight states (<a href="/wiki/Arkansas" title="Arkansas">Arkansas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maryland" title="Maryland">Maryland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts" title="Massachusetts">Massachusetts</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Carolina" title="North Carolina">North Carolina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Carolina" title="South Carolina">South Carolina</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tennessee" title="Tennessee">Tennessee</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Texas" title="Texas">Texas</a>) also contain clauses that prohibit atheists from holding public office.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> However, these clauses were held by the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">U.S. Supreme Court</a> to be unenforceable in the 1961 case of <i><a href="/wiki/Torcaso_v._Watkins" title="Torcaso v. Watkins">Torcaso v. Watkins</a></i>, where the court ruled unanimously that such clauses constituted a religious test incompatible with the religious test prohibition in <a href="/wiki/No_religious_test_clause" class="mw-redirect" title="No religious test clause">Article 6 Section 3</a> of the United States Constitution.\n\nThe <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Hawaii" title="Church of Hawaii">Church of Hawaii</a> was the state church of Hawaii from 1862\xe2\x80\x931893.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-128"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-128">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The <a href="/wiki/Church_in_Wales" title="Church in Wales">Church in Wales</a> was split from the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a> in 1920, by <a href="/wiki/Welsh_Church_Act_1914" title="Welsh Church Act 1914">Welsh Church Act 1914</a>; at the same time becoming disestablished.</span>\n</li>\n</ol></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=State_religion&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">\n<div class="mw-references-wrap mw-references-columns"><ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><i>The Journal of Ecclesiastical History</i>. p. 268 by Cambridge University Press, Gale Group, C.W. Dugmore</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-2">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The headship was administrative and jurisdictional but did not include the <i>potestas ordinis</i> (the right to preach, ordain, administer the sacraments and rites of the Church which were reserved to the clergy). Bray, Gerald. <i>Documents of the English Reformation</i> James Clarke &amp; C\xc2\xba (1994), p. 114</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-3">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Neill, Stephen. <i>Anglicanism</i> Penguin (1960), p. 61</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-4">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The concerned religious communities are the <a href="/wiki/Diocese_of_Metz" class="mw-redirect" title="Diocese of Metz">dioceses of Metz</a> and <a href="/wiki/Archdiocese_of_Strasbourg" class="mw-redirect" title="Archdiocese of Strasbourg">of Strasbourg</a>, the Lutheran <a href="/wiki/EPCAAL" class="mw-redirect" title="EPCAAL">EPCAAL</a> and the Reformed <a href="/wiki/EPRAL" class="mw-redirect" title="EPRAL">EPRAL</a> and the three <a href="/wiki/Consistory_(Judaism)" title="Consistory (Judaism)">Israelite consistories</a> in Colmar, Metz and Strasbourg.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-5">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20071127125443/http://www.constitution.bt/draft_constitution_3rd_en.pdf">"Draft of Tsa Thrim Chhenmo"</a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span>. www.constitution.bt. 1 August 2007. Archived from <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.constitution.bt/draft_constitution_3rd_en.pdf">the original</a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span> on 27 November 2007<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">18 October</span> 2007</span>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Draft+of+Tsa+Thrim+Chhenmo&amp;rft.pub=www.constitution.bt&amp;rft.date=2007-08-01&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.constitution.bt%2Fdraft_constitution_3rd_en.pdf&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AState+religion" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r861714446"/>\n<dl><dd>Article 3, Spiritual Heritage\n<ol><li>Buddhism is the spiritual heritage of Bhutan, which promotes the principles and values of peace, non-violence, compassion and tolerance.</li>\n<li>The <i><a href="/wiki/Druk_Gyalpo" title="Druk Gyalpo">Druk Gyalpo</a></i> is the protector of all religions in Bhutan.</li>\n<li>It shall be the responsibility of religious institutions and personalities to promote the spiritual heritage of the country while also ensuring that religion remains separate from politics in Bhutan. Religious institutions and personalities shall remain above politics.</li>\n<li>The <i>Druk Gyalpo</i> shall, on the recommendation of the Five <i>Lopon</i>s, appoint a learned and respected monk ordained in accordance with the <i>Druk-lu</i>, blessed with the nine qualities of a spiritual master and accomplished in <i>ked-dzog</i>, as the <i><a href="/wiki/Je_Khenpo" title="Je Khenpo">Je Khenpo</a></i>.</li>\n<li>His Holiness the <i>Je Khenpo</i> shall, on the recommendation of the <i><a href="/wiki/Dratshang_Lhentshog" title="Dratshang Lhentshog">Dratshang Lhentshog</a></i>, appoint monks blessed with the nine qualities of a spiritual master and accomplished in <i>ked-dzog</i> as the Five <i>Lopon</i>s.</li>\n<li>The members of the <i>Dratshang Lhentshog</i> shall comprise:<br /><span style="padding-left:2em;">&#160;</span>(a) The <i>Je Khenpo</i> as Chairman;<br /><span style="padding-left:2em;">&#160;</span>(b) The Five <i>Lopon</i>s of the <i>Zhung Dratshang</i>; and<br /><span style="padding-left:2em;">&#160;</span>(c) The Secretary of the <i>Dratshang Lhentshog</i> who is a civil servant.</li>\n<li>The <i>Zhung Dratshang</i> and <i>Rabdeys</i> shall continue to receive adequate funds and other facilities from the State.<cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://constituteproject.org/constitution/Bhutan_2008.pdf?lang=en">"Bhutan\'s Constitution of 2008"</a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span>. <i>constituteproject.org/</i><span class="reference-accessdate">. Official relations of the republic with the Holy See shall be governed by concordats or other bilateral agreements." The 1992 Constitution, which replaced the 1967 one, establishes Paraguay as a secular state, as mentioned in section (1) of Article 24: "Freedom of religion, worship, and ideology is recognized without any restrictions other than those established in this Constitution and the law.
